```spl2
-- Recipe 01: Hello World
-- Minimal SPL program — verify spl2 + adapter + model work.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl
--   spl2 run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl2 run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3

PROMPT hello_world
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Introduce yourself and SPL 2.0 in two sentences.')
GENERATE greeting()
```

```spl2
-- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl2 run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl2 run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl2 run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

```spl2
-- Recipe 03: Multilingual Greeting
-- Greet in any language — demonstrates parametric context with lang.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Chinese"
--   spl2 run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="French"
--   spl2 run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Japanese"
--   spl2 run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Spanish"

PROMPT multilingual_greeting
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Respond in the language specified.'),
    context.user_input AS input,
    context.lang AS lang
GENERATE greeting(input, lang)
```

```spl2
-- Recipe 04: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl2 run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce each model's raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi3',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    COMMIT @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4

[05] Self-Refine

```spl2
-- SPL 2.0: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT: 
    @task text,
    @max_iterations integer default 5
  OUTPUT: @result text
DO
  @iteration := 0
  --  @max_iterations := 5

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) INTO @current

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    GENERATE critique(@current) INTO @feedback

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN 'satisfactory' THEN
        COMMIT @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  COMMIT @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 6

```output
	+ "Logic's gentle pulse beats through circuits"
	+ "Mind in digital dreams"
```

```spl2
-- SPL 2.0: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.
$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  COMMIT @result
END

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.
$$;

WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT: 
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr integer default 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1
  -- @year_curr := 2024

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 186 in / 105 out
     | Latency: 34467ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

This represents a year-over-year growth rate of 0.33%, reflecting a slow but steady demographic expansion. Such subdued growth is consistent with broader trends across Western Europe, where aging populations and low fertility rates temper overall population gains.

```spl2
-- SPL 2.0: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'refined'
    OTHERWISE
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    COMMIT @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 968 in / 847 out
     | Latency: 10725ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Encryption is the process of converting plaintext (readable data) into ciphertext (unreadable data) to protect it from unauthorized access. Here's a step-by-step explanation of how encryption works:

1. **Key Generation**: The encryption algorithm uses a secret key, which is a mathematical concept that can be used to encrypt and decrypt data.
2. **Data Preparation**: The plaintext data is prepared for encryption by converting it into a format that the encryption algorithm can understand.
3. **Encryption Process**: The encryption algorithm applies a series of transformations to the plaintext data using the secret key. These transformations may include:
	* Substitution: Replacing certain characters or bits with others.
	* Permutation: Rearranging the order of characters or bits.
	* Addition or substitution with other values (e.g., XOR).
4. **Ciphertext Generation**: The encrypted data, known as ciphertext, is generated by applying these transformations to the plaintext data.
5. **Key Management**: The secret key used for encryption is kept confidential and secure, as it can be used to decrypt the data.
6. **Decryption Process**: To decrypt the ciphertext, the recipient uses the same encryption algorithm and secret key (but in reverse). This transforms the encrypted data back into its original form, known as plaintext.

Types of Encryption:

1. **Symmetric Encryption**: Uses the same key for both encryption and decryption (e.g., AES).
2. **Asymmetric Encryption**: Uses a pair of keys: a public key for encryption and a private key for decryption (e.g., RSA).

Common Encryption Techniques:

1. **Block Ciphers**: Divide data into fixed-length blocks, encrypting each block independently.
2. **Stream Ciphers**: Encrypt data one bit or byte at a time.
3. **Hash Functions**: One-way functions that take input data and produce a fixed-size output (e.g., SHA-256).

In summary, encryption works by using a secret key to transform plaintext data into ciphertext, making it unreadable without the correct decryption key.
```

```spl2
-- Recipe 08: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install numpy faiss-cpu
--   spl2 rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl2 run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl2 run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl2 run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl2 run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)
```

```spl2
-- Recipe 09: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl2 run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    COMMIT @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 1252 in / 1486 out
     | Latency: 19242ms

```output
The article provides an in-depth analysis of distributed AI inference, covering its strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and threats. The key points are summarized as follows:

**Strengths:**

* Scalability: Distributed AI inference can process large amounts of data across multiple machines.
* Flexibility: Deployment on various devices, including smartphones, tablets, and edge computing devices.
* Speed: Faster computation and prediction times due to distributed processing.

**Weaknesses:**

* Communication Overhead: Latency and overhead introduced by communication between machines.
* Data Synchronization: Ensuring all machines have synchronized data is a significant challenge.
* Model Consistency: Maintaining consistency across multiple models trained on different machines or datasets is essential.

**Opportunities:**

* Edge Computing: Performing AI inference near the source of data, reducing latency and improving real-time processing capabilities.
* Specialized Hardware: Graphics Processing Units (GPUs), Tensor Processing Units (TPUs), and Application-Specific Integrated Circuits (ASICs) offer significant performance improvements for AI inference.

**Threats:**

* Security Risks: Vulnerability to security risks, such as data breaches or model tampering.
* Interoperability Issues: Different machines or devices may have varying levels of compatibility.

**Recommendations:**

* Invest in Edge Computing to reduce latency and improve real-time processing capabilities.
* Develop Secure AI Models using techniques such as encryption, secure protocols, and model validation.
* Implement Model Consistency Checks regularly.

The article concludes that distributed AI inference offers benefits but also presents challenges. By understanding these points, developers can build robust and efficient AI applications that meet the demands of real-world problems.
```

     | 
     | Distributed AI inference refers to the process of using multiple computers or devices to perform complex machine learning tasks, such as deep learning and neural networks. This approach is designed to scale up the power of artificial intelligence (AI) by harnessing the collective processing capabilities of many machines.
     | 
     | **Benefits of Distributed AI Inference**
     | 
     | 1. **Scalability**: Distributed AI inference allows for the processing of large amounts of data across multiple machines, making it an ideal solution for big data applications.
     | 2. **Flexibility**: By using a distributed architecture, AI models can be deployed on a variety of devices, including smartphones, tablets, and edge computing devices.
     | 3. **Speed**: Distributed processing enables faster computation and prediction times, which is critical for real-time applications such as autonomous vehicles and smart cities.
     | 4. **Fault Tolerance**: With multiple machines working together, distributed AI inference provides a redundant system that can continue to function even if one or more machines fail.
     | 
     | **Technologies Used in Distributed AI Inference**
     | 
     | 1. **Distributed Training**: Techniques such as model parallelism, data parallelism, and gradient averaging are used to train large machine learning models across multiple machines.
     | 2. **Cloud Computing**: Cloud providers such as Amazon Web Services (AWS), Microsoft Azure, and Google Cloud Platform (GCP) offer scalable infrastructure for distributed AI inference.
     | 3. **Edge Computing**: Edge computing devices, such as smart routers or network switches, can be used to perform AI inference near the source of data, reducing latency and improving real-time processing capabilities.
     | 4. **Specialized Hardware**: Graphics Processing Units (GPUs), Tensor Processing Units (TPUs), and Application-Specific Integrated Circuits (ASICs) are designed specifically for AI inference and can provide significant performance improvements.

[10] Batch Test
     cmd : spl2 run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
     log : /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test_20260320_192156.log

```bash
spl2 run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3
```
     | 
```spl2
-- Recipe 10: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl2 run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 7
     | Tokens: 550 in / 282 out
     | Latency: 4204ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

```output
  PASS  02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl (llama3.2)
```

     | 
```spl2
-- Recipe 11: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl2 run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl2 run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds integer DEFAULT 3
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    -- @max_rounds := 3
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history + '\n---\n' + @pro_rebuttal

        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history + '\n---\n' + @con_rebuttal

        @round := @round + 1
    END

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 12107 in / 5042 out
     | Latency: 80990ms

```output
Here is the rewritten text:

**Opening Statement**

The development and deployment of Artificial Intelligence (AI) technologies have far-reaching implications for various industries and aspects of our lives. As we continue to advance in this field, it's essential that we consider the ethics of open-sourcing AI.

**Arguments For Open-Sourcing AI:**

1.  **Transparency and Trust**: By making AI code available, developers can ensure that the algorithms and models used are transparent, understandable, and trustworthy.
2.  **Collaboration and Innovation**: Open-sourcing AI enables researchers, engineers, and organizations to work together, share ideas, and accelerate innovation.
3.  *Cost Savings*: Open-source AI projects can reduce development costs by leveraging collective resources and expertise.
4.  *Improved Security*: With multiple eyes on the code, vulnerabilities can be identified and addressed more quickly.

**Arguments Against Open-Sourcing AI:**

1.  **National Security Risks**: Sensitive information, such as encryption methods or proprietary algorithms, may compromise national security if exposed through open-sourcing.
2.  *Commercial Competitiveness*: Companies that invest heavily in AI research and development may feel compelled to keep their work proprietary to maintain a competitive edge.
3.  *Data Privacy Concerns*: Open-sourcing AI can put sensitive data, such as personal identifiable information (PII) or protected health information (PHI), at risk of unauthorized access or misuse.
4.  *Intellectual Property Issues*: Open-sourcing AI may infringe on existing patents or intellectual property rights, leading to costly litigation and disputes.

The following points provide additional context:

1.  **Regulatory Frameworks**: Establishing regulatory frameworks can help address national security concerns while still promoting transparency and innovation.
2.  *Access Control Measures*: Implementing access control measures, such as encryption and secure authentication protocols, can mitigate data privacy risks.
3.  *Commercial Alternatives*: Companies can explore alternative strategies, such as licensing AI technologies or offering subscription-based services, to maintain competitiveness.

The following points provide a conclusion to the argument:

1.  The debate surrounding open-sourcing AI is complex and multifaceted.
2.  While there are valid arguments for and against open-sourcing AI, it's essential to strike a balance between promoting transparency, collaboration, and innovation while addressing concerns over national security, commercial competitiveness, and data privacy.
3.  Open-sourcing AI can promote transparency, collaboration, and innovation, which are essential components of a thriving and innovative economy.

The following additional resources provide further information on the topic:

1.  *AI Open-Source Initiative*: A project aimed at promoting the development of open-source AI tools.
2.  *European Union's General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR)*: A comprehensive data protection regulation that sets standards for handling personal identifiable information and protected health information in AI applications.
3.  *National Security Agency's (NSA) Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning*: A program that focuses on developing secure and trustworthy AI systems.

These resources can provide valuable insights into the benefits and challenges of open-sourcing AI, as well as practical examples of how to implement this approach in various contexts.
```

     | 
     | The development and deployment of Artificial Intelligence (AI) technologies have far-reaching implications for various industries and aspects of our lives. As we embark on this journey, it's essential to consider the ethics of open-sourcing AI. On one hand, open-sourcing AI can promote transparency, collaboration, and innovation. On the other hand, it may compromise national security, hinder commercial competitiveness, or put sensitive information at risk.
     | 
     | **Arguments For Open-Sourcing AI:**
     | 
     | 1.  **Transparency and Trust**: By making AI code available, developers can ensure that the algorithms and models used are transparent, understandable, and trustworthy. This can foster trust among end-users and promote a culture of openness.
     | 2.  **Collaboration and Innovation**: Open-sourcing AI enables researchers, engineers, and organizations to work together, share ideas, and accelerate innovation. This collaborative approach can lead to groundbreaking discoveries and applications that benefit society as a whole.
     | 3.  **Cost Savings**: Open-source AI projects can reduce development costs by leveraging collective resources and expertise. This can be particularly beneficial for small businesses or startups that may not have the necessary funding to develop their own AI solutions.
     | 4.  **Improved Security**: With multiple eyes on the code, vulnerabilities can be identified and addressed more quickly. This can lead to a safer and more secure AI ecosystem.
     | 
     | **Arguments Against Open-Sourcing AI:**
     | 
     | 1.  **National Security Risks**: Sensitive information, such as encryption methods or proprietary algorithms, may compromise national security if exposed through open-sourcing. For instance, the Chinese government's alleged exploitation of US intellectual property raises concerns about the risks of open-sourcing AI.
     | 2.  **Commercial Competitiveness**: Companies that invest heavily in AI research and development may feel compelled to keep their work proprietary to maintain a competitive edge. This can stifle innovation and limit access to cutting-edge technology.
     | 3.  **Data Privacy Concerns**: Open-sourcing AI can put sensitive data, such as personal identifiable information (PII) or protected health information (PHI), at risk of unauthorized access or misuse. For example, the Cambridge Analytica scandal highlights the importance of protecting user data in AI applications.
     | 4.  **Intellectual Property Issues**: Open-sourcing AI may infringe on existing patents or intellectual property rights, leading to costly litigation and disputes. This can create uncertainty and hinder innovation.
     | 
     | **Counterarguments**
     | 
     | 1.  **Regulatory Frameworks**: Establishing regulatory frameworks can help address national security concerns while still promoting transparency and innovation. For instance, the EU's General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) sets clear guidelines for data protection in AI applications.
     | 2.  **Access Control Measures**: Implementing access control measures, such as encryption and secure authentication protocols, can mitigate data privacy risks. This can ensure that sensitive information remains protected while still allowing for collaboration and innovation.
     | 3.  **Commercial Alternatives**: Companies can explore alternative strategies, such as licensing AI technologies or offering subscription-based services, to maintain competitiveness without compromising national security or data privacy.
     | 
     | **Conclusion**
     | 
     | The debate surrounding open-sourcing AI is complex and multifaceted. While there are valid arguments for and against open-sourcing AI, it's essential to strike a balance between promoting transparency, collaboration, and innovation while addressing concerns over national security, commercial competitiveness, and data privacy. By considering multiple perspectives and exploring alternative approaches, we can create a more nuanced understanding of the benefits and risks associated with open-sourcing AI.
     | 
     | This revised script incorporates some suggested improvements, including:
     | 
     | 1.  **More nuanced language**: The script uses more precise language to describe the potential risks and benefits of open-sourcing AI.
     | 2.  **Counterexamples or opposing views**: The script includes examples of how open-sourcing AI has worked in practice, such as the EU's GDPR, to strengthen its argument.
     | 3.  **Multiple perspectives**: The script acknowledges alternative viewpoints on the topic, such as concerns about national security risks or commercial competitiveness.
     | 4.  **Long-term implications**: The script explores the potential long-term consequences of open-sourcing AI, such as its impact on future technological advancements.
     | 
     | Overall, this revised script provides a more comprehensive and balanced argument in favor of making AI open-sourced.
     | ---

     |   @pro = **Argument in Favor of Making AI Open-Sourced**
     | 
     | Ladies and gentlemen, today I want to discuss a crucial aspect of artificial intelligence that has far-reaching implications for the future of technology. As we continue to develop and deploy AI systems, it is essential that we consider making them open-sourced. This approach will not only promote transparency but also foster innovation, collaboration, and trust in the field.
     | 
     | **Argument 1: Promoting Transparency**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI systems means making their underlying code, data, and research accessible to anyone who wants to study or work on them. This increased transparency has several benefits:
     | 
     | *   It allows developers to review and audit the code, ensuring that it is secure and free from biases.
     | *   Researchers can build upon existing work, accelerating progress in AI development.
     | *   The public can gain a better understanding of how AI systems are trained and deployed.
     | 
     | **Argument 2: Encouraging Collaboration**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI systems creates opportunities for collaboration among developers, researchers, and policymakers. When the code is available, people from different backgrounds and industries can work together:
     | 
     | *   Sharing knowledge and expertise to improve AI systems.
     | *   Developing new applications and use cases.
     | *   Addressing concerns and biases in AI decision-making.
     | 
     | **Argument 3: Fostering Innovation**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI systems enables innovation by providing a platform for developers to build upon existing work. This leads to:
     | 
     | *   The creation of new applications and services that benefit society.
     | *   Improved solutions to real-world problems.
     | *   Increased competition, driving down costs and improving efficiency.
     | 
     | **Argument 4: Building Trust**
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI systems demonstrates trust in the technology by making it vulnerable to scrutiny. When developers can review the code and research:
     | 
     | *   They can identify potential biases or security vulnerabilities.
     | *   The public has confidence that AI systems are being developed with their best interests in mind.
     | 
     | In conclusion, open-sourcing AI systems is a crucial step towards promoting transparency, encouraging collaboration, fostering innovation, and building trust. By making AI open-sourced, we can create a more equitable, efficient, and trustworthy technology that benefits society as a whole.

     | 3. **Cost Savings**: Open-source AI projects can reduce development costs by leveraging collective resources and expertise.
     | 4. **Improved Security**: With multiple eyes on the code, vulnerabilities can be identified and addressed more quickly.
     | 
     | **Arguments Against Open-Sourcing AI:**
     | 
     | 1. **National Security Risks**: Sensitive information, such as encryption methods or proprietary algorithms, may compromise national security if exposed through open-sourcing.
     | 2. **Commercial Competitiveness**: Companies that invest heavily in AI research and development may feel compelled to keep their work proprietary to maintain a competitive edge.
     | 3. **Data Privacy Concerns**: Open-sourcing AI can put sensitive data, such as personal identifiable information (PII) or protected health information (PHI), at risk of unauthorized access or misuse.
     | 4. **Intellectual Property Issues**: Open-sourcing AI may infringe on existing patents or intellectual property rights, leading to costly litigation and disputes.
     |

     |

     | 2. **Access Control Measures**: Implementing access control measures, such as encryption and secure authentication protocols, can mitigate data privacy risks.
     | 3. **Commercial Alternatives**: Companies can explore alternative strategies, such as licensing AI technologies or offering subscription-based services, to maintain competitiveness.
     | 
     | **Conclusion**
     | 
     | The debate surrounding open-sourcing AI is complex and multifaceted. While there are valid arguments for and against open-sourcing AI, it is essential to strike a balance between promoting transparency, collaboration, and innovation while addressing concerns over national security, commercial competitiveness, and data privacy. A nuanced approach that incorporates regulatory frameworks, access control measures, and alternative strategies can help mitigate risks and unlock the full potential of AI.

[12] Plan and Execute
     cmd : spl2 run ./12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama task=Build a REST API for a todo app
     log : /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute_20260320_192321.log
     | 
```bash
spl2 run ./12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 task=Build a REST API for a todo app
```
     | 
```spl2
-- Recipe 12: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a complex task into steps, then an executor runs each step.
-- Demonstrates agentic planning, sequential execution, and progress tracking.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 task="Build a REST API for a todo app"
--   spl2 run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"
--   spl2 run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama task="Migrate a MySQL database to PostgreSQL"

WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
    INPUT: @task TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
DO
    @step_index := 0
    @results := ''

    -- Phase 1: Planning — decompose task into numbered steps
    GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan

    -- Phase 2: Count steps in plan
    GENERATE count_steps(@plan) INTO @step_count

    -- Phase 3: Execute each step sequentially
    WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
        -- Extract current step from plan
        GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step

        -- Execute the step with context from prior results
        GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result

        -- Validate step output
        GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation

        EVALUATE @validation
            WHEN 'failed' THEN
                -- Re-plan remaining steps given the failure
                GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
                GENERATE count_steps(@plan) INTO @step_count
                @step_index := 0
                @results := ''
            OTHERWISE
                @results := @results + '\n## Step ' + @step_index + '\n' + @step_result
                @step_index := @step_index + 1
        END
    END

    -- Phase 4: Synthesize final report
    GENERATE synthesize(@task, @results) INTO @final_report

    COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE synthesize(@task, @results) INTO @final_report
        COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```output
Based on the input "Build a REST API for a todo app", here's a synthesis of what that might entail:

**Overview**

The task involves creating a REST (Representational State of Resource) based API to manage tasks and users in a todo application. The API will provide endpoints for CRUD (Create, Read, Update, Delete) operations on tasks and users.

**Endpoints**

1. **GET /tasks**: Retrieves all tasks for the authenticated user.
2. **POST /tasks**: Creates a new task with the provided details.
3. **GET /tasks/{id}**: Retrieves a single task by its ID.
4. **PUT /tasks/{id}**: Updates an existing task with the provided details.
5. **DELETE /tasks/{id}**: Deletes a task by its ID.

6. **GET /users**: Retrieves all users for the todo application.
7. **POST /users**: Creates a new user with the provided details.
8. **GET /users/{id}**: Retrieves a single user by their ID.
9. **PUT /users/{id}**: Updates an existing user with the provided details.
10. **DELETE /users/{id}**: Deletes a user by their ID.

**Authentication**

The API will require authentication for all endpoints. Users can be authenticated using JSON Web Tokens (JWT) or Basic Auth.

**Data Models**

1. **Task Model**
   - `id` (int): Unique task identifier.
   - `title` (str): Task title.
   - `description` (str): Task description.
   - `completed` (bool): Indicates whether the task is completed.

2. **User Model**
   - `id` (int): Unique user identifier.
   - `username` (str): User username.
   - `email` (str): User email address.

**Database**

The API will interact with a relational database management system (RDBMS) such as MySQL or PostgreSQL, using SQL to perform CRUD operations on tasks and users.

**API Framework**

A suitable API framework for building this REST API could be:

- Flask in Python
- Express.js in Node.js

**Testing**

To ensure the API is working correctly, unit tests and integration tests will need to be written to cover each endpoint.
```

```javascript
// Import required modules
const pedometer = require('pedometerjs');
const readline = require('readline');

// Initialize the pedometer object
let pedometerInstance;

function initPedometer() {
  // Set up the interval to update the step count every second
  pedometerInstance = new pedometer.Pedometer();
  pedometerInstance.updateInterval = 1000; // Update every second

  // Start updating the step count
  pedometerInstance.start();

  // Get the readline interface
  const rl = readline.createInterface({
    input: process.stdin,
    output: process.stdout
  });

  // Prompt the user to press Enter when they want to stop tracking steps
  rl.question('Press Enter when you want to stop tracking steps... ', (input) => {
    pedometerInstance.stop();

    // Close the readline interface
    rl.close();
  });
}

// Get the current step count and display it on the console
function getAndDisplayStepCount() {
  if (!pedometerInstance || !pedometerInstance.isRunning()) {
    console.log('No pedometer instance is running.');
  } else {
    const stepCount = pedometerInstance.getDistance();
    console.log(`You have walked ${stepCount} steps.`);
  }
}

// Initialize the pedometer
initPedometer();

// Set an interval to display the current step count every minute
setInterval(getAndDisplayStepCount, 60000);
```
     | 
     | **How it works:**
     | 
     | 1. The `pedometer` module is used to create a pedometer instance that tracks the user's steps.
     | 2. When you run this code, it creates a new instance of the pedometer and sets an interval to update the step count every second.
     | 3. Once the pedometer starts updating, you press Enter when you want to stop tracking your steps.
     | 4. The `getAndDisplayStepCount` function gets the current step count using the pedometer's getDistance method and displays it on the console.
     | 
     | **Example usage:**
     | 
     | To run this code, save it in a file named `pedometer.js`, then execute it with Node.js:
     | 
```bash
node pedometer.js
```

     | When you run the code, it will prompt you to press Enter when you want to stop tracking steps. Once you do, it displays your current step count on the console.
     | 
     | **Note:** This is a basic example to get you started with counting steps using a pedometer. Depending on your requirements, you may need to add more features or modify this code to suit your needs.

```spl2
-- Recipe 13: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl2 run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl2 run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT: @document TEXT, @style TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := ''

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document
    GENERATE chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        GENERATE extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        @summaries := @summaries + '\n[Chunk ' + @chunk_index + ']: ' + @chunk_summary
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — combine all chunk summaries into one
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries) INTO @final_summary
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- If document is too large even for chunking, do hierarchical reduction
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries, @style) INTO @final_summary
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```python
import re

def improve_summary(text):
    """
    Improves the summary of the given text by removing unnecessary characters.

    Args:
        text (str): The input text to be improved.

    Returns:
        str: The improved summary.
    """

    # Remove bullet points
    text = re.sub(r'^\* ', '', text, flags=re.MULTILINE)
    
    # Remove line breaks and other whitespace characters
    text = re.sub(r'\s+', ' ', text)
    
    # Remove leading and trailing whitespace characters
    text = text.strip()
    
    return text

# Testing the function
text1 = """
Hello World!
* Item 1
* Item 2
"""

print(improve_summary(text1))

text2 = """
This is a bullet point:
* Item 1
* Item 2
"""

print(improve_summary(text2))
```

```python
import re

def improve_summary(text: str) -> str:
    """
    Improves the summary of the given text by removing unnecessary characters.

    Args:
        text (str): The input text to be improved.

    Returns:
        str: The improved summary.
    """

    # Remove bullet points, line breaks and other whitespace characters
    text = re.sub(r'^\*|\s+', '', text, flags=re.MULTILINE)

    # Remove leading and trailing whitespace characters
    text = text.strip()

    return text

# Testing the function
text1 = """
Hello World!
* Item 1
* Item 2
"""

print(improve_summary(text1))

text2 = """
This is a bullet point:
* Item 1
* Item 2
"""

print(improve_summary(text2))
```

     | 3. Used the `strip()` method to remove leading and trailing whitespace characters instead of `text = text.strip()`.

     |   @score = Based on the provided input, it appears that you want to create a function called `quality_score` that calculates a score based on the quality of a given text summary. However, there is no clear indication of what metrics or criteria this score should be calculated using.
     | 
     | To make an educated guess, I will assume that you want to calculate a score based on the following factors:
     | 
     | 1.  The length of the original document and the summary.
     | 2.  The number of unique words in both the original document and the summary.
     | 3.  The similarity between the original document and the summary.
     | 
     | Here is an example implementation for the `quality_score` function:
     | 
```python
import re
from collections import Counter

def reduce_summaries(text):
    # Remove bullet points
    text = re.sub(r'^\* ', '', text, flags=re.MULTILINE)
    
    # Remove line breaks and other whitespace characters
    text = re.sub(r'\s+', ' ', text)
    
    # Remove leading and trailing whitespace characters
    text = text.strip()
    
    return text

def quality_score(original_text, reduced_text):
    # Calculate the length ratio of original document to summary
    length_ratio = len(original_text) / len(reduced_text)
    
    # Calculate the similarity between the two texts using Jaccard Similarity
    words_original = Counter(reduced_summaries(original_text).lower().split())
    words_summary = Counter(reduced_summaries(reduced_text).lower().split())
    jaccard_similarity = sum(min(words_original[word], words_summary[word]) for word in set(list(words_original.keys()) + list(words_summary.keys()))) / len(set(list(words_original.keys()) + list(words_summary.keys())))
    
    # Calculate the quality score
    quality_score = (length_ratio * 0.3) + jaccard_similarity * 0.7
    
    return quality_score

# Testing the function
text1 = """
Hello World!
* Item 1
* Item 2
"""
print(reduce_summaries(text1))

text2 = """
This is a bullet point:
* Item 1
* Item 2
"""

print(quality_score(text1, text2))
```
     | 
     | In this example, the `quality_score` function calculates a score based on the length ratio of the original document to the summary and the Jaccard similarity between the two texts. The scores are then combined using a weighted average.
     | 
     | Note that this is just one possible implementation, and you may need to adjust or modify it to suit your specific requirements.

```spl2
-- Recipe 14: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl2 run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl2 run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts + '\n\nKey Themes:\n' + @themes
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' + @trends + '\n\nRisks:\n' + @risks + '\n\nOpportunities:\n' + @opportunities
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    COMMIT @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

```output
**Revised Report: Impact of AI on Healthcare**

**Executive Summary:**
The integration of Artificial Intelligence (AI) in healthcare has been gaining momentum, transforming the way medical professionals diagnose and treat patients. This report provides an in-depth analysis of the impact of AI on healthcare, highlighting its benefits, challenges, trends, risks, and opportunities.

**Introduction:**
The healthcare sector is one of the most complex systems, requiring the integration of multiple technologies to improve patient outcomes. Artificial Intelligence (AI) has been increasingly applied in healthcare, leveraging machine learning algorithms to analyze large amounts of medical data, identify patterns, and make predictions. This report aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the impact of AI on healthcare.

**Benefits of AI in Healthcare:**

1. **Improved Diagnostic Accuracy**: AI-powered diagnostic tools have been shown to improve accuracy in certain medical conditions, such as cancer detection.
2. **Enhanced Patient Outcomes**: AI can help personalize treatment plans and improve patient engagement through virtual assistants and chatbots.
3. **Reduced Costs**: AI can automate administrative tasks, reducing the time and resources spent on data entry and other manual processes.
4. **Increased Accessibility**: AI-powered solutions can provide remote access to healthcare services, improving accessibility for patients with mobility issues or living in rural areas.
5. **Better Decision-Making**: AI can analyze large amounts of medical data to identify patterns and make predictions, enabling better decision-making.

**Challenges and Limitations of AI in Healthcare:**

1. **Data Quality and Availability**: Inconsistent or missing data can lead to inaccurate diagnoses, ineffective treatment plans, and compromised patient outcomes.
2. **Regulatory Frameworks**: Lack of clear regulations and standards for AI development and deployment can result in non-compliance, reputational damage, and potential legal issues.
3. **Interoperability between Systems**: Inadequate integration of AI systems with existing healthcare infrastructure can lead to data silos, reduced efficiency, and decreased patient outcomes.
4. **Liability and Accountability**: Unclear liability and accountability frameworks for AI-driven decisions can lead to confusion, mistrust, and potential lawsuits.
5. **Patient Trust and Acceptance**: Patients may be hesitant to adopt AI-powered solutions due to concerns about data privacy and security.

**Trends:**

1. **Increased Adoption of AI-Powered Solutions**: AI is being increasingly adopted in healthcare, transforming traditional practices.
2. **Focus on Patient Outcomes**: The importance of patient outcomes is growing, with AI-powered solutions focusing on improving diagnosis accuracy, treatment efficacy, and patient engagement.
3. **Integration with Existing Healthcare Systems**: Interoperability between AI systems and existing healthcare infrastructure is crucial for maximizing benefits.
4. **Addressing Regulatory and Security Challenges**: Clear regulatory frameworks and robust cybersecurity measures are essential to ensure safe and responsible AI adoption in healthcare.
5. **Increased Emphasis on Cybersecurity**: Cybersecurity threats pose a significant risk, emphasizing the need for robust protection measures.

**Emerging Trends:**

1. **Increased use of Natural Language Processing (NLP)**: NLP is being explored for its potential applications beyond diagnosis and treatment, such as patient engagement and medical research.
2. **Expansion into New Areas**: AI is being explored for new applications in population health management and medical research.

**Recommendations:**
Based on the analysis, the following recommendations are made:

1. Establish clear regulatory frameworks and standards for AI development and deployment in healthcare.
2. Implement robust cybersecurity measures to protect sensitive medical data from unauthorized access.
3. Ensure transparency and accountability in AI-driven decision-making processes.
4. Conduct regular audits and evaluations to identify and address potential biases in AI algorithms.
5. Provide patient education and awareness about the benefits and limitations of AI-powered healthcare solutions.

**Risks:**
The analysis identified high-risk areas, including:

1. **Data Quality and Availability**: Inconsistent or missing data can lead to inaccurate diagnoses, ineffective treatment plans, and compromised patient outcomes.
2. **Regulatory Frameworks**: Lack of clear regulations and standards for AI development and deployment can result in non-compliance, reputational damage, and potential legal issues.
3. **Interoperability between Systems**: Inadequate integration of AI systems with existing healthcare infrastructure can lead to data silos, reduced efficiency, and decreased patient outcomes.
4. **Liability and Accountability**: Unclear liability and accountability frameworks for AI-driven decisions can lead to confusion, mistrust, and potential lawsuits.

**Moderate-Risk Areas:**

1. **Cybersecurity Threats**: AI-powered systems are vulnerable to cyber threats, which can compromise sensitive medical data and disrupt patient care.
2. **Bias in Decision-Making**: AI algorithms can perpetuate existing biases if trained on biased data or designed without diversity and inclusivity in mind.

**Low-Risk Areas:**

1. **Improved Diagnostic Accuracy**: AI-powered diagnostic tools have
```

     | Opportunities:
     | def find_opportunities(facts, themes):
     |     # Initialize a dictionary to store opportunities
     |     opportunities = {
     |         "Healthcare Outcomes": [],
     |         "Automation and Efficiency": [],
     |         "Personalization and Customization": [],
     |         "Predictive Analytics and Risk Management": [],
     |         "Medical Technology Innovation": [],
     |         "Cybersecurity and Data Protection": []
     |     }
     | 
     |     # Iterate through the facts dictionary
     |     for fact in facts:
     |         # Check if the fact is related to a theme
     |         for theme in themes:
     |             # If the fact contains the theme, add it to the opportunities list
     |             if theme.lower() in fact.lower():
     |                 if theme not in opportunities:
     |                     opportunities[theme] = []
     |                 opportunities[theme].append(fact)
     | 
     |     return opportunities
     | 
     | # Example usage
     | facts = [
     |     "AI-powered algorithms can analyze large amounts of medical data, including images and patient histories, to help doctors make more accurate diagnoses.",
     |     "AI can help personalize medicine by analyzing a patient's genetic profile, medical history, and lifestyle factors to recommend tailored treatment plans.",
     |     "AI-powered predictive analytics can identify high-risk patients and predict the likelihood of adverse events, such as hospital readmissions or complications.",
     |     # Add more facts here...
     | ]
     | 
     | themes = [
     |     "Improving Healthcare Outcomes",
     |     "Automation and Efficiency",
     |     "Personalization and Customization",
     |     "Predictive Analytics and Risk Management",
     |     "Medical Technology Innovation",
     |     "Cybersecurity and Data Protection"
     | ]
     | 
     | opportunities = find_opportunities(facts, themes)
     | 
     | # Print the opportunities
     | for theme, opportunity_list in opportunities.items():
     |     print(theme)
     |     for fact in opportunity_list:
     |         print(fact)
     |     print()

```spl2
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl2 run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl2 run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT: @code TEXT, @language TEXT
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```

```output
def foo(x):
    """Evaluates the input string as a Python literal structure.
    
    Args:
        x (str): Input to evaluate
    
    Returns:
        The parsed value as a Python object
    
    Raises:
        ValueError: If the input is invalid or unparseable
    """

    try:
        # Use ast.literal_eval() to safely parse the input string
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    
    except (ValueError, TypeError):
        # Handle cases where the input cannot be parsed as a Python literal structure
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```

```python
print(foo("[1, 2, 3]"))  # Output: [1, 2, 3]
print(foo("'Hello, World!'"))  # Output: 'Hello, World!'
```

     | **Security Review**
     | 
     | The provided code snippet is a simple function `foo` that takes a string `x` as input and returns the result of evaluating it using the built-in `eval()` function.
     | 
     | ### Vulnerability Analysis
     | 
     | *   **Use of `eval()`:**
     |    - The use of `eval()` can pose security risks if the input is not properly sanitized, as it can evaluate any Python expression.
     |    - This makes it vulnerable to code injection attacks.
     | 
     | ### Recommendations for Improvement
     | 
     | 1.  **Replace `eval()` with Safe Alternatives:**
     |     *   Consider using safer alternatives like `ast.literal_eval()`, `json.loads()`, or `safe_eval()` from the `aseval` library to evaluate and parse strings.
     | 2.  **Implement Secure Coding Practices:**
     |     *   Ensure all code follows secure coding practices, such as validating user input, avoiding unexpected input, and using whitelisting instead of blacklisting.
     | 
     | ### Revised Code
     | 
     | Instead of using `eval()`, consider using `ast.literal_eval()` to safely evaluate strings:
     |

```python
def foo(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except ValueError:
        # Handle invalid or unparseable input
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```

     | *   Use safe and efficient alternatives like `ast.literal_eval()` instead of `eval()`.
     | *   Follow PEP 8 naming conventions for function names (lowercase with underscores).
     | *   Include docstrings or comments to explain the purpose and behavior of functions.

     | Input 10:
     | # Severity Score for Vulnerability to Code Injection Attacks
     | 
     | ## Description
     | 
     | The severity score for the vulnerability to code injection attacks is **CRITICAL**.
     | 
     | ## Explanation
     | 
     | The use of `eval()` in the `foo` function poses a significant security risk, allowing an attacker to execute arbitrary Python code and making it vulnerable to code injection attacks. This is a critical issue that requires immediate attention and remediation.
     | 
     | ## Score Breakdown
     | 
     | *  
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @code = def foo(x): return eval(x)
     |   @language = Python
     |   @security_findings = **Security Audit Report**
     | 
     | The provided code snippet `def foo(x): return eval(x)` is a potential security risk due to the use of the `eval()` function.
     | 
     | ### Vulnerability Analysis
     | 
     | The `eval()` function evaluates a string as Python code and returns the result. In this case, the input `x` is directly passed to `eval()`. This allows an attacker to inject arbitrary Python code by manipulating the value of `x`.
     | 
     | **Example Attack Scenario:**
     | 
     | * An attacker sends a crafted input `x = "1; print('Hello, World!'); exit()"`
     | * When `foo()` is called with this input, it will execute the injected Python code, printing "Hello, World!" and exiting the program
     | 
     | ### Recommendations for Improvement
     | 
     | To mitigate this security risk, consider using safer alternatives to `eval()`, such as:
     | 
     | 1. **`ast.literal_eval()`**: Evaluates a string as a Python literal structure (e.g., list, tuple, dict) and returns it as a Python object.
     | 2. **`json.loads()`**: Parses a JSON-formatted string into a Python dictionary.
     | 3. **`parse.quote()`**: Escapes special characters in a string to prevent injection attacks.
     | 
     | Here's an updated implementation using `ast.literal_eval()`:
```python
import ast

def foo(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, TypeError):
        # Handle invalid or unparseable input
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```
     | **Additional Recommendations:**
     | 
     | * Always validate and sanitize user-input data to prevent injection attacks.
     | * Consider using a more robust security framework, such as OWASP's CSRF protection library.

     |   @bug_findings = **Bug Detection**
     | 
     | The given function `foo` is vulnerable to a severe security risk due to the use of the `eval()` function. The `eval()` function allows executing arbitrary Python code, which can lead to:
     | 
     | 1. **Code Injection**: An attacker could inject malicious Python code as input to the `foo` function.
     | 2. **Denial of Service (DoS)**: Malicious input could cause the function to execute an infinite loop or consume excessive resources, leading to a DoS attack.
     | 3. **Data Tampering**: Malicious input could allow unauthorized modification of internal data structures.
     | 
     | **Example Vulnerability**
     | 
```python
foo('1 + 1')  # Returns 2
foo('x = 5; x')  # Sets an external variable and returns None
```
     | 
     | In the second example, `eval()` executes the malicious code `'x = 5; x'`, allowing an attacker to modify external data.
     | 
     | **Recommendation**
     | 
     | To address this vulnerability, we should avoid using `eval()` in favor of safer alternatives. In Python, consider using:
     | 
     | 1. **`compile()`**: Compile a string into a bytecode object without executing it immediately.
     | 2. **`ast.literal_eval()`**: Safely evaluate literals (e.g., strings, numbers) from untrusted input.
     | 
     | Here's an updated version of the `foo` function that uses `compile()` to achieve similar functionality:
     | 
```python
def foo(x):
    try:
        return compile(x, '', 'eval').co_value
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error: {e}")
        return None
```
     | 
     | **Best Practice**
     | 
     | Always prefer safer alternatives like `compile()` over `eval()`. If you must use `eval()`, make sure to:
     | 
     | 1. Validate the input extensively.
     | 2. Use a whitelisting approach (only allow trusted inputs).
     | 3. Consider using a safer alternative, like `ast.literal_eval()`, for literals.
     | 
     | **Code Quality**
     | 
     | To further improve code quality:
     | 
```python
def foo(x):
    """Safely evaluates a string as Python code"""
    try:
        return compile(x, '', 'eval').co_value
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error: {e}")
        return None

# Example usage
result = foo('1 + 1')
print(result)  # Output: 2
```
     | 
     | This revised version includes:
     | 
     | 1. A clear docstring explaining the function's purpose.
     | 2. Improved error handling and feedback.
     | 3. Consistent naming conventions.
     | 
     | By addressing this security vulnerability, you've improved the overall reliability and maintainability of your codebase.
     |   @sec_score = As the task is to improve the provided Python function `foo(x)` which uses the `eval()` function due to a potential security risk.      | 
```python
import ast

def foo(x):
    """Evaluates the input string as a Python literal structure.
    
    Args:
        x (str): Input to evaluate
    
    Returns:
        The parsed value as a Python object
    
    Raises:
        ValueError: If the input is invalid or unparseable
    """

    try:
        # Use ast.literal_eval() to safely parse the input string
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    
    except (ValueError, TypeError):
        # Handle cases where the input cannot be parsed as a Python literal structure
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```
     | This updated implementation ensures that only valid Python literals are executed. The usage example remains the same:
     | 
```python

print(foo("[1, 2, 3]"))  # Output: [1, 2, 3]
print(foo("'Hello, World!'"))  # Output: 'Hello, World!'
```

```spl2
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl2 run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl2 run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @max_reflections := 3
    @confidence := 0

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            OTHERWISE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer

                @iteration := @iteration + 1
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```

**Designing a URL Shortener System**

The goal is to create a user-friendly and efficient URL shortener system. Here's a design for such a system:

**System Requirements**

1.  **User Input**: The system should accept long URLs as input from users.
2.  **Slug Generation**: The system should generate a unique slug (shortened URL) for each submitted URL.
3.  **Database Storage**: The system should store the original URL and its corresponding shortened URL in a database.
4.  **Redirect Functionality**: The system should redirect users to the original URL when they visit the shortened URL.

**System Components**

1.  **Frontend**: A web interface for users to submit URLs and access their shortened URLs.
2.  **Backend**: A server-side application that handles user input, generates slugs, stores data in the database, and provides redirect functionality.
3.  **Database**: A storage solution for the system's data (original URLs and their corresponding shortened URLs).

**System Flow**

1.  User submits a long URL through the frontend interface.
2.  The backend application receives the input URL and generates a unique slug using a hashing algorithm.
3.  The backend application stores the original URL and its corresponding shortened URL in the database.
4.  The system redirects users to the original URL when they visit the shortened URL.

**Database Schema**

The following database schema can be used to store the system's data:

```sql
CREATE TABLE urls (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    original_url VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    slug VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    created_at TIMESTAMP NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);
```
     | 
     | This schema includes fields for the original URL, shortened URL (slug), and a timestamp for when each entry was added to the database.
     | 
     | **Slug Generation Algorithm**
     | 
     | A simple hashing algorithm can be used to generate unique slugs. Here's an example implementation:
     | 
```python
import hashlib

def generate_slug(original_url):
    # Hash the original URL using SHA-256
    hashed_url = hashlib.sha256(original_url.encode()).hexdigest()
    
    # Append a UUID to the hash
    slug = f"{hashed_url}-{uuid.uuid4().hex[:6]}"
    
    return slug
```
     | 
     | This algorithm takes an original URL, hashes it using SHA-256, and appends a unique identifier (UUID) to the resulting hash. The result is a unique slug that can be used as the shortened URL.
     | 
     | **System Advantages**
     | 
     | 1.  **Convenience**: Users can easily share long URLs by providing them with their shortened versions.
     | 2.  **Storage Efficiency**: The system reduces storage space required for storing original URLs, making it more efficient.
     | 3.  **Scalability**: The system can handle a large number of users and submissions without significant performance degradation.
     | 
     | **System Disadvantages**
     | 
     | 1.  **Security Risks**: If not implemented properly, the system may be vulnerable to security risks such as SQL injection or cross-site scripting (XSS).
     | 2.  **Data Overload**: The system may become overwhelmed with a large number of submissions if not designed with scalability in mind.
     | 
     | **System Best Practices**
     | 
     | 1.  **Use Secure Algorithms**: Use secure hashing algorithms like SHA-256 or BLAKE2 to reduce the risk of security breaches.
     | 2.  **Implement Data Validation**: Validate user input data (URLs) to prevent potential security risks.
     | 3.  **Optimize Database Performance**: Optimize database performance by using indexing, caching, and other techniques to ensure efficient storage and retrieval of data.

```python
import flask
from flask import request, redirect, url_for
import uuid
import hashlib

app = flask.Flask(__name__)

def generate_slug(original_url):
    # Hash the original URL using SHA-256
    hashed_url = hashlib.sha256(original_url.encode()).hexdigest()
    
    # Append a UUID to the hash
    slug = f"{hashed_url}-{uuid.uuid4().hex[:6]}"
    
    return slug

@app.route('/shorten', methods=['POST'])
def shorten_url():
    original_url = request.form['original_url']
    
    if not original_url:
        return 'Invalid URL', 400
    
    # Generate a unique slug for the URL
    slug = generate_slug(original_url)
    
    # Store the original URL and its corresponding shortened URL in the database
    db[slug] = original_url
    
    return {'shortened_url': f'/{slug}'}

if __name__ == '__main__':
    app.run(debug=True)
```
     | 
     | This updated version of the code uses secure hashing algorithms (`hashlib.sha256`) to generate unique slugs and includes data validation to prevent potential security risks.
