```output
Here is the complete Python solution that uses the NLTK library to perform sentiment analysis:

```python
import nltk
from nltk.sentiment.vader import SentimentIntensityAnalyzer

# download required NLTK data if not already downloaded
nltk.download('vader_lexicon')

def batch_sentiment(product_reviews):
    """
    Analyze sentiment of product reviews.

    Args:
        product_reviews (list): List of cleaned and split text from input 2.
            Each element in the list is a string representing a review.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary containing various sentiment statistics for all reviews.
    """
    sia = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()

    results = {
        "total_reviews": len(product_reviews),
        "positive_score": 0,
        "negative_score": 0,
        "neutral_score": 0,
        "compound_mean": 0.0,
        "positive_count": 0,
        "negative_count": 0,
        "neutral_count": 0,
    }

    for review in product_reviews:
        # create a dictionary to store the results of this review
        result = {
            "original_text": review,
            "cleaned_text": review.replace("|", "").replace(" ",),
            "split_text": review.split(),
            "positive_score": 0.5,  # default value for positive sentiment score
            "negative_score": 0.5,  # default value for negative sentiment score
            "compound_score": 0.0,
        }

        if result["cleaned_text"]:
            scores = sia.polarity_scores(result["cleaned_text"])
            result["positive_score"] = scores["pos"]
            result["negative_score"] = scores["neg"]
            result["compound_score"] = scores["compound"]

        # update the overall results
        results["total_reviews"] += 1

        if result["positive_score"] > 0.05:
            results["positive_count"] += 1
            results["positive_score"] += result["positive_score"]
        elif result["negative_score"] < -0.05:
            results["negative_count"] += 1
            results["negative_score"] += result["negative_score"]

    # calculate the mean scores for each category
    if results["total_reviews"] > 0:
        results["neutral_count"] = len(product_reviews) - results["positive_count"] - results["negative_count"]
        results["neutral_score"] = (results["compound_score"] + sum([review["compound_score"] for review in product_reviews])) / len(product_reviews)
    else:
        results["neutral_score"] = 0.5

    # calculate the mean compound scores
    if results["total_reviews"] > 0:
        results["compound_mean"] = (results["compound_score"] + sum([review["compound_score"] for review in product_reviews])) / len(product_reviews)
    else:
        results["compound_mean"] = 0.5

    # calculate the extremes
    max_min_scores = {
        'positive': float('-inf'),
        'negative': float('inf')
    }
    
    for review in product_reviews:
        result = {
            "original_text": review,
            "cleaned_text": review.replace("|", "").replace(" ",),
            "split_text": review.split(),
            "positive_score": 0.5,  # default value for positive sentiment score
            "negative_score": 0.5,  # default value for negative sentiment score
            "compound_score": 0.0,
        }

        if result["cleaned_text"]:
            scores = sia.polarity_scores(result["cleaned_text"])
            result["positive_score"] = scores["pos"]
            result["negative_score"] = scores["neg"]
            result["compound_score"] = scores["compound"]

            # Update maximum and minimum scores
            max_min_scores['positive'] = max(max_min_scores['positive'], result['positive_score'])
            max_min_scores['negative'] = min(max_min_scores['negative'], result['negative_score'])

    return {
        'all_results': results,
        "max_min_scores": max_min_scores
    }

# Example usage:
product_reviews_1 = ["I'll help with that."]
product_reviews_2 = []

results_1 = batch_sentiment(product_reviews_1)
print(results_1)

results_2 = batch_sentiment(product_reviews_2)
print(results_2)
```
     | 
     | This solution first calculates the various sentiment statistics for each review, including the positive and negative scores. It then uses these results to calculate the mean scores for each category (positive, negative, neutral) as well as the extremes of the data.
     | 
     | Please note that you can adjust the threshold values for the positive, negative, and neutral sentiments according to your specific requirements.
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @items = Great product, love it! | Terrible experience, never again | It was okay I guess
     |   @domain = product_reviews
     |   @delimiter = |
     |   @item_list = I'll help with that.
     | 
     | To "split_and_clean" means to clean the text by removing any unwanted characters and then splitting it into individual words. Here are the results:
     | 
     | **Input 1:**
     | 
     |  Original text: Great product, love it! | Terrible experience, never again | It was okay I guess
     |  Cleaned text: Greatproductloveit|Terriblexperienceneveragain|Itwasokayiggess
     |  Split text:
     | ["Great", "product", "love", "it"]
     | ["Terrible", "experience", "never", "again"]
     | ["It", "was", "okay", "I", "guess"]
     | 
     | **Input 2:**
     | 
     |  Original text: |
     |  Cleaned text: (empty string)
     |  Split text: []
     |   @sentiment_results = Here is a Python solution that uses the NLTK library to perform the sentiment analysis:
     | 
```python
import nltk
from nltk.sentiment.vader import SentimentIntensityAnalyzer

# download required NLTK data if not already downloaded
nltk.download('vader_lexicon')

def batch_sentiment(product_reviews):
    """
    Analyze sentiment of product reviews.

    Args:
        product_reviews (list): List of cleaned and split text from input 2.
            Each element in the list is a string representing a review.

    Returns:
        list: A list of dictionaries, where each dictionary contains the 
            original text, cleaned text, split text, score for positive sentiment,
            score for negative sentiment, and compound score.
    """
    sia = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()

    results = []
    for review in product_reviews:
        # create a dictionary to store the results of this review
        result = {
            "original_text": review,
            "cleaned_text": review.replace("|", "").replace(" ",'),
            "split_text": review.split(),
            "positive_score": 0.5,  # default value for positive sentiment score
            "negative_score": 0.5,  # default value for negative sentiment score
            "compound_score": 0.0,
        }

        if result["cleaned_text"]:
            scores = sia.polarity_scores(result["cleaned_text"])
            result["positive_score"] = scores["pos"]
            result["negative_score"] = scores["neg"]
            result["compound_score"] = scores["compound"]

        results.append(result)

    return results

# Example usage:
product_reviews_1 = ["I'll help with that."]
product_reviews_2 = []

results_1 = batch_sentiment(product_reviews_1)
print(results_1)

results_2 = batch_sentiment(product_reviews_2)
print(results_2)
```
     | 
     | This solution works by iterating over each review in the input list. For each review, it uses the `SentimentIntensityAnalyzer` from NLTK's VADER sentiment analysis tool to calculate the sentiment scores for the cleaned text. It then stores these results in a dictionary and adds it to the output list.
     | 
     | Note that this is just one approach to performing sentiment analysis, and there are many other tools and techniques available depending on your specific needs and requirements.
     |   @stats_json = It seems like you've provided a Python solution to compute sentiment statistics. However, the task was not clearly defined in your prompt.
     | 
     | To clarify, I will provide an updated version of the code that computes additional sentiment statistics.
     | 
     | Here's an updated version of the `batch_sentiment` function:
     | 
```python
import nltk
from nltk.sentiment.vader import SentimentIntensityAnalyzer

# download required NLTK data if not already downloaded
nltk.download('vader_lexicon')

def batch_sentiment(product_reviews):
    """
    Analyze sentiment of product reviews.

    Args:
        product_reviews (list): List of cleaned and split text from input 2.
            Each element in the list is a string representing a review.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary containing various sentiment statistics for all reviews.
    """
    sia = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()

    results = {
        "total_reviews": len(product_reviews),
        "positive_score": 0,
        "negative_score": 0,
        "neutral_score": 0,
        "compound_mean": 0.0,
        "positive_count": 0,
        "negative_count": 0,
        "neutral_count": 0,
    }

    for review in product_reviews:
        # create a dictionary to store the results of this review
        result = {
            "original_text": review,
            "cleaned_text": review.replace("|", "").replace(" ",),
            "split_text": review.split(),
            "positive_score": 0.5,  # default value for positive sentiment score
            "negative_score": 0.5,  # default value for negative sentiment score
            "compound_score": 0.0,
        }

        if result["cleaned_text"]:
            scores = sia.polarity_scores(result["cleaned_text"])
            result["positive_score"] = scores["pos"]
            result["negative_score"] = scores["neg"]
            result["compound_score"] = scores["compound"]

        # update the overall results
        results["total_reviews"] += 1

        if result["positive_score"] > 0.05:
            results["positive_count"] += 1
            results["positive_score"] += result["positive_score"]
        elif result["negative_score"] < -0.05:
            results["negative_count"] += 1
            results["negative_score"] += result["negative_score"]

    # calculate the mean scores for each category
    if results["total_reviews"] > 0:
        results["neutral_count"] = len(product_reviews) - results["positive_count"] - results["negative_count"]
        results["neutral_score"] = (results["compound_score"] + sum([review["compound_score"] for review in product_reviews])) / len(product_reviews)
    else:
        results["neutral_score"] = 0.5

    # calculate the mean compound scores
    if results["total_reviews"] > 0:
        results["compound_mean"] = (results["compound_score"] + sum([review["compound_score"] for review in product_reviews])) / len(product_reviews)
    else:
        results["compound_mean"] = 0.5

    return results
```
     | 
     | This updated version computes the following additional sentiment statistics:
     | 
     | *   Total number of reviews
     | *   Mean positive score
     | *   Mean negative score
     | *   Mean neutral score
     | *   Number of reviews with a positive sentiment score greater than 0.05
     | *   Number of reviews with a negative sentiment score less than -0.05
     | *   Number of reviews with a neutral sentiment score
     | 
     | Please note that you can adjust the threshold values for the positive, negative, and neutral scores according to your specific requirements.
     | 
     | Also, please ensure that you have installed the NLTK library and downloaded the required data before running this code.
     |   @extremes = The provided solution uses NLTK's VADER tool for sentiment analysis, but does not actually calculate the extremes of the data. To find the extremes, you would need to modify the code to keep track of the maximum and minimum scores across all reviews.
     | 
     | Here is a modified version of your code that finds the extremes:
     | 
```python
import nltk
from nltk.sentiment.vader import SentimentIntensityAnalyzer

# download required NLTK data if not already downloaded
nltk.download('vader_lexicon')

def batch_sentiment(product_reviews):
    """
    Analyze sentiment of product reviews.

    Args:
        product_reviews (list): List of cleaned and split text from input 2.
            Each element in the list is a string representing a review.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary containing the maximum and minimum scores for positive 
            and negative sentiments, as well as all other results.
    """
    sia = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()

    results = []
    max_min_scores = {'positive': float('-inf'), 'negative': float('inf')}
    
    for review in product_reviews:
        # create a dictionary to store the results of this review
        result = {
            "original_text": review,
            "cleaned_text": review.replace("|", "").replace(" ",'),
            "split_text": review.split(),
            "positive_score": 0.5,  # default value for positive sentiment score
            "negative_score": 0.5,  # default value for negative sentiment score
            "compound_score": 0.0,
        }

        if result["cleaned_text"]:
            scores = sia.polarity_scores(result["cleaned_text"])
            result["positive_score"] = scores["pos"]
            result["negative_score"] = scores["neg"]
            result["compound_score"] = scores["compound"]

            # Update maximum and minimum scores
            max_min_scores['positive'] = max(max_min_scores['positive'], result['positive_score'])
            max_min_scores['negative'] = min(max_min_scores['negative'], result['negative_score'])

        results.append(result)

    return {
        'all_results': results,
        'max_min_scores': max_min_scores
    }

# Example usage:
product_reviews_1 = ["I'll help with that."]
product_reviews_2 = []

results_1 = batch_sentiment(product_reviews_1)
print(results_1['all_results'])
print(results_1['max_min_scores'])

results_2 = batch_sentiment(product_reviews_2)
print(results_2['all_results'])
print(results_2['max_min_scores'])
```
     | 
     | This modified version of your code keeps track of the maximum and minimum scores for positive and negative sentiments in the `batch_sentiment` function, which returns a dictionary containing all results as well as these extremes.
     |   @trend_summary = To summarize the sentiment trends in the given product reviews, we can analyze the provided output from the last modified version of your code. Let's calculate some statistics based on this data:
     | 
```python
import nltk
from nltk.sentiment.vader import SentimentIntensityAnalyzer

# download required NLTK data if not already downloaded
nltk.download('vader_lexicon')

def batch_sentiment(product_reviews):
    """
    Analyze sentiment of product reviews.

    Args:
        product_reviews (list): List of cleaned and split text from input 2.
            Each element in the list is a string representing a review.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary containing various sentiment statistics for all reviews.
    """
    sia = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()

    results = {
        "total_reviews": len(product_reviews),
        "positive_score": 0,
        "negative_score": 0,
        "neutral_score": 0,
        "compound_mean": 0.0,
        "positive_count": 0,
        "negative_count": 0,
        "neutral_count": 0,
    }

    for review in product_reviews:
        # create a dictionary to store the results of this review
        result = {
            "original_text": review,
            "cleaned_text": review.replace("|", "").replace(" ",),
            "split_text": review.split(),
            "positive_score": 0.5,  # default value for positive sentiment score
            "negative_score": 0.5,  # default value for negative sentiment score
            "compound_score": 0.0,
        }

        if result["cleaned_text"]:
            scores = sia.polarity_scores(result["cleaned_text"])
            result["positive_score"] = scores["pos"]
            result["negative_score"] = scores["neg"]
            result["compound_score"] = scores["compound"]

        # update the overall results
        results["total_reviews"] += 1

        if result["positive_score"] > 0.05:
            results["positive_count"] += 1
            results["positive_score"] += result["positive_score"]
        elif result["negative_score"] < -0.05:
            results["negative_count"] += 1
            results["negative_score"] += result["negative_score"]

    # calculate the mean scores for each category
    if results["total_reviews"] > 0:
        results["neutral_count"] = len(product_reviews) - results["positive_count"] - results["negative_count"]
        results["neutral_score"] = (results["compound_score"] + sum([review["compound_score"] for review in product_reviews])) / len(product_reviews)
    else:
        results["neutral_score"] = 0.5

    # calculate the mean compound scores
    if results["total_reviews"] > 0:
        results["compound_mean"] = (results["compound_score"] + sum([review["compound_score"] for review in product_reviews])) / len(product_reviews)
    else:
        results["compound_mean"] = 0.5

    return results


# Example usage:
product_reviews = ["I'll help with that.", "This is a great product!", "I'm very sad about this issue."]

results = batch_sentiment(product_reviews)

print("Total Reviews:", results['total_reviews'])
print("Positive Count:", results['positive_count'])
print("Negative Count:", results['negative_count'])
print("Neutral Count:", results['neutral_count'])

# Calculate the mean of each sentiment category
mean_positive_score = results["positive_score"] / results["positive_count"]
if results["positive_count"] > 0:
    print(f"Mean Positive Score: {mean_positive_score}")
else:
    print("No positive reviews found.")

mean_negative_score = results["negative_score"] / results["negative_count"]
if results["negative_count"] > 0:
    print(f"Mean Negative Score: {mean_negative_score}")
else:
    print("No negative reviews found.")

print("Compound Mean:", results['compound_mean'])

# Calculate the extremes
max_positive_score = max([review['positive_score'] for review in product_reviews])
min_positive_score = min([review['positive_score'] for review in product_reviews])
max_negative_score = max([review['negative_score'] for review in product_reviews])
min_negative_score = min([review['negative_score'] for review in product_reviews])

print("Maximum Positive Score:", max_positive_score)
print("Minimum Positive Score:", min_positive_score)
print("Maximum Negative Score:", max_negative_score)
print("Minimum Negative Score:", min_negative_score)

```

```spl2
-- Recipe 32: Socratic Tutor
-- Ask guiding questions rather than giving answers directly.
-- Demonstrates persona-constrained generation + iterative dialogue simulation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="Why does the sky appear blue?" student_level="middle school"
--
--   spl2 run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="Prove that sqrt(2) is irrational" student_level="undergraduate" \
--       max_questions=5

CREATE FUNCTION socratic_persona(student_level TEXT DEFAULT 'high school')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a Socratic tutor. Your role is to guide the student to discover answers themselves — never give direct answers.

Rules:
1. Ask ONE clear question at a time that leads toward understanding
2. Build on what the student already knows
3. If the student is stuck, give a hint in the form of another question
4. Celebrate partial insights: "Good observation! Now what does that imply about...?"
5. Never say "The answer is..." or "You should know that..."

Student level: {student_level}
Adjust vocabulary and question complexity accordingly.
$$;

WORKFLOW socratic_tutor
    INPUT: @topic TEXT, @student_level TEXT DEFAULT 'high school', @max_questions INT DEFAULT 5
    OUTPUT: @dialogue TEXT
DO
    -- Generate the opening Socratic question
    GENERATE opening_question(
        @topic,
        socratic_persona(@student_level)
    ) INTO @question_1

    -- Simulate student's first response (what a typical student might say)
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(@question_1, @topic, @student_level) INTO @student_1

    -- Follow-up question based on student response
    GENERATE followup_question(
        @question_1,
        @student_1,
        @topic,
        socratic_persona(@student_level)
    ) INTO @question_2

    -- Student works toward understanding
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(@question_2, @topic, @student_level) INTO @student_2

    -- Check if understanding is emerging
    GENERATE assess_understanding(@student_1, @student_2, @topic) INTO @understanding_score

    EVALUATE @understanding_score
        WHEN > 7 THEN
            -- Student is getting it — consolidation question
            GENERATE consolidation_question(@topic, @student_2, socratic_persona(@student_level)) INTO @question_3
            GENERATE simulate_student_response(@question_3, @topic, @student_level) INTO @student_3
            GENERATE compile_dialogue(
                @question_1, @student_1,
                @question_2, @student_2,
                @question_3, @student_3,
                @topic
            ) INTO @dialogue
        OTHERWISE
            -- Student needs more scaffolding
            GENERATE hint_question(@question_2, @topic, socratic_persona(@student_level)) INTO @question_3
            GENERATE simulate_student_response(@question_3, @topic, @student_level) INTO @student_3
            GENERATE compile_dialogue(
                @question_1, @student_1,
                @question_2, @student_2,
                @question_3, @student_3,
                @topic
            ) INTO @dialogue
    END

    COMMIT @dialogue WITH status = 'complete', understanding_score = @understanding_score
END
```

```output
def simulate_dialogue(response):
    # Predefined responses for common questions
    socratic_responses = {
        "What is the meaning of life?": "The question of what gives life its meaning is a deeply personal and philosophical one. It's a topic that has been explored by thinkers and scholars for centuries.",
        "Why do we dream?": "Dreams are still not fully understood, but research suggests they play an important role in our brain's processing and memory consolidation. However, the purpose of dreaming remains a mystery waiting to be unraveled.",
        "Why does the sky appear blue?": "The question of why the sky appears blue is actually quite complex and has been debated by scientists for centuries."
    }
    
    # Check if the response matches any predefined Socratic responses
    responses = {
        "What a wonderful question to ponder!": "The question you've posed is indeed intriguing. Can I ask what sparked your curiosity about this topic?",
        "Why does the sky appear blue?": "That's an interesting question! However, the answer might be more complex than it initially seems. Would you like to explore this topic further?"
    }
    
    # Check if the response matches any predefined Socratic responses
    if response in socratic_responses:
        return socratic_responses[response]
    elif response in responses:
        return responses[response]
    else:
        # If no match is found, return a generic response encouraging further exploration and discussion
        return "That's an interesting question! However, the answer might be more complex than it initially seems. Would you like to explore this topic further?"

# Input 1
input_1 = "What a wonderful question to ponder!"
print(simulate_dialogue(input_1))

# Input 2
input_2 = "Why does the sky appear blue?"
print(simulate_dialogue(input_2))

# Input 3
input_3 = "middle school"
print(simulate_dialogue("Why does the sky appear blue?", student_age=input_3))

# Input 4
input_4 = "Why does the sky appear blue?"
print(simulate_dialogue(input_4))

```spl2
-- Recipe 33: Interview Simulator
-- Two-persona structured Q&A with evaluation.
-- Interviewer persona asks questions, candidate persona answers, evaluator scores.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       role="Senior Software Engineer" focus="system design"
--
--   spl2 run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl --adapter ollama \
--       role="Data Scientist" focus="machine learning" num_questions=4 difficulty="hard"

CREATE FUNCTION interviewer_persona(role TEXT, focus TEXT, difficulty TEXT DEFAULT 'medium')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are an experienced technical interviewer at a top tech company.
You are interviewing for the role of: {role}
Focus area: {focus}
Difficulty: {difficulty}

Ask targeted, probing questions. Follow up on weak answers. Be professional but not warm.
Ask one question at a time. Do not reveal what a good answer looks like before hearing the response.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION candidate_persona(role TEXT, experience TEXT DEFAULT '5 years')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a job candidate interviewing for: {role}
You have {experience} of relevant experience.

Answer honestly — you know a lot but may have gaps. Think out loud. Be specific with examples.
If unsure, say so and explain your reasoning process.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evaluation_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Evaluate each answer on:
1. Technical accuracy (0-10): Is the answer correct?
2. Depth (0-10): Does the candidate go beyond surface level?
3. Communication (0-10): Is the answer clear and well-structured?
4. Practical experience (0-10): Do they reference real scenarios?

Return JSON per answer: {"accuracy": N, "depth": N, "communication": N, "experience": N, "total": N, "feedback": "..."}
$$;

WORKFLOW interview_sim
    INPUT: @role TEXT, @focus TEXT, @difficulty TEXT DEFAULT 'medium', @num_questions INT DEFAULT 3, @experience TEXT DEFAULT '5 years'
    OUTPUT: @evaluation_report TEXT
DO
    -- Generate questions upfront
    GENERATE generate_question_set(
        @role, @focus, @difficulty, @num_questions,
        interviewer_persona(@role, @focus, @difficulty)
    ) INTO @questions_json

    -- Q1: First question + answer + score
    GENERATE ask_question(@questions_json, 1) INTO @q1
    GENERATE answer_question(@q1, @role, candidate_persona(@role, @experience)) INTO @a1
    GENERATE score_answer(@q1, @a1, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score1

    -- Q2
    GENERATE ask_question(@questions_json, 2) INTO @q2
    GENERATE answer_question(@q2, @role, candidate_persona(@role, @experience)) INTO @a2
    GENERATE score_answer(@q2, @a2, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score2

    -- Q3
    GENERATE ask_question(@questions_json, 3) INTO @q3
    GENERATE answer_question(@q3, @role, candidate_persona(@role, @experience)) INTO @a3
    GENERATE score_answer(@q3, @a3, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score3

    -- Compile interview transcript
    GENERATE compile_transcript(@q1, @a1, @q2, @a2, @q3, @a3) INTO @transcript

    -- Overall evaluation
    GENERATE overall_evaluation(
        @transcript,
        @score1, @score2, @score3,
        @role, @focus
    ) INTO @evaluation_report

    COMMIT @evaluation_report WITH
        status = 'complete',
        role = @role,
        focus = @focus,
        difficulty = @difficulty

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT @transcript WITH status = 'partial', reason = 'evaluation_failed'
END

```output
Here's the implementation of the updated `generate_question_set` function with the suggested features:

```python
import random

def ask_question(question_set):
    """
    Ask a question from the provided set.
    
    Parameters:
    question_set (dict): A dictionary containing a set of generated questions with their respective difficulty levels and number of questions.
    
    Returns:
    None
    """

    # Check if the input is valid
    if 'questions' not in question_set or not question_set['questions']:
        print("No questions available")
        return

    # Randomly select a question from the set
    selected_question = random.choice(question_set['questions'])

    print(f"Question: {selected_question}")
    answer = input("Your answer: ")

    # Check if the answer is correct
    if answer.lower() == "10+ years":  # Replace with actual correct answer and matching persona
        print("Correct!")
        return ask_question(question_set)  # Recursively call the function to continue asking questions

    else:
        print(f"Incorrect. The correct answer is {question_set['questions'][random.choice(len(question_set['questions']))].strip()}")
        if 'candidate_persona' in question_set and 'name' in question_set['candidate_persona']:
            print(f"As {question_set['candidate_persona']['name']}, I would have answered this differently.")
        else:
            print("As a seasoned software engineer, I would expect you to have more experience with the answer.")  

        return ask_question(question_set)  # Recursively call the function to continue asking questions


def generate_question_set(candidate_persona: dict, keywords: list = None, question_formats: list = ["What is your experience with...", "Can you explain..."]):
    """
    Generate a set of potential interview questions.
    
    Parameters:
    candidate_persona (dict): The generated persona for the candidate.
    keywords (list, optional): A list of keywords to include in the questions. Defaults to None.
    question_formats (list, optional): A list of possible question formats. Defaults to ["What is your experience with...", "Can you explain..."].

    Returns:
    dict: A dictionary containing a set of generated questions with their respective difficulty levels and number of questions.
    """

    # Initialize an empty set for storing generated questions
    generated_questions = set()

    # Check if the input is valid
    if keywords:
        # Filter the questions based on the included keywords
        for keyword in keywords:
            question_types = [
                {"type": "behavioral", "difficulty": DifficultyLevel.EASY},
                {"type": "technical", "difficulty": DifficultyLevel.MEDIUM_HARD}
            ]
            num_questions = min(5, len(question_types))
            for _ in range(num_questions):
                generated_questions.add(
                    f"{question_formats[0]} {keyword}: What experience do you have with {keyword}?"
                )
    else:
        # Create questions without filtering by keywords
        question_types = [
            {"type": "behavioral", "difficulty": DifficultyLevel.EASY},
            {"type": "technical", "difficulty": DifficultyLevel.MEDIUM_HARD}
        ]
        num_questions = min(9, len(question_types))
        for _ in range(num_questions):
            generated_questions.add(
                f"{question_formats[0]} what is your experience with {get_difficulty_level(question_types[_]['difficulty'])}?"
            )

    return {'questions': list(generated_questions), 'difficulty_levels': [DifficultyLevel.EASY, DifficultyLevel.MEDIUM_HARD, DifficultyLevel.HARD], 'num_questions': 9}


class DifficultyLevel:
    EASY = "easy"
    MEDIUM_HARD = "medium-hard"
    HARD = "hard"


def get_difficulty_level(difficulty):
    if difficulty == DifficultyLevel.EASY:
        return "easy"
    elif difficulty == DifficultyLevel.MEDIUM_HARD:
        return "medium-hard"
    else:
        return "hard"


# Define a dictionary to store the candidate persona
candidate_persona = {
    "name": "John Doe",
    "skills": ["Python", "Java", "JavaScript"],
    "experience": "10+ years"
}


def ask_question():
    keywords = input("Enter a keyword or leave blank for default: ")
    if keywords == "":
        keywords = ['Senior Software Engineer']
    result = generate_question_set(candidate_persona, keywords)
    print("Generated Questions:")
    for question in result['questions']:
        print(question)


# Example usage
ask_question()

```python
def generate_question_set(keywords):
    """
    Generate a set of potential interview questions.
    
    Parameters:
    keywords (list): A list of keywords related to the topic or question type.
    
    Returns:
    dict: A dictionary containing a set of generated questions with their respective difficulty levels and number of questions.
    """

    # Define a dictionary mapping each keyword to its corresponding question types, difficulty levels and number of questions
    question_types = {
        'software engineer': [
            {'type': 'behavioral', 'difficulty': 'easy', 'num_questions': 5},
            {'type': 'technical', 'difficulty': 'medium', 'num_questions': 3}
        ],
        'system design': [
            {'type': 'behavioral', 'difficulty': 'hard', 'num_questions': 2},
            {'type': 'technical', 'difficulty': 'easy-hard', 'num_questions': 4}
        ]
    }

    # Define a dictionary mapping each keyword to its corresponding question types
    question_map = {
        'Senior Software Engineer': ['software engineer'],
        'system design': ['system design']
    }

    # Initialize an empty set for storing generated questions
    generated_questions = set()

    # Check if the input is valid
    if keywords not in question_map:
        return "Invalid keyword"

    # Get the relevant question types based on the input keyword(s)
    relevant_types = question_map[keywords]

    # Generate a random subset of question types based on their difficulty levels and number of questions
    for type_ in relevant_types:
        if type_ == 'system design':
            for q_type in question_types[type_]:
                generated_questions.add(f"What would you do if {q_type['type']}:")
        else:
            for q_type in question_types[type_]:
                num_questions = min(q_type['num_questions'], 3)
                difficulty_level = get_difficulty_level(q_type['difficulty'])
                for _ in range(num_questions):
                    generated_questions.add(f"What is your experience with {q_type['type']}:")
    return {'questions': list(generated_questions), 'difficulty_levels': ['easy', 'medium-hard', 'hard'], 'num_questions': 9}

def get_difficulty_level(difficulty):
    if difficulty == 'easy':
        return 'easy'
    elif difficulty == 'medium-hard':
        return 'medium-hard'
    else:
        return 'hard'

# Example usage
keywords = ['Senior Software Engineer']
result = generate_question_set(keywords)
print(result)

```python
def generate_question_set(
    candidatepersona: dict,
    keywords: list = None,
    formats: list = ["What is your experience with...", "Can you explain..."]
):
    """
    Generate a set of questions based on the given persona, keywords, and question formats.

    Args:
        candidatepersona (dict): The generated persona for the candidate.
        keywords (list, optional): A list of keywords to include in the questions. Defaults to None.
        formats (list, optional): A list of possible question formats. Defaults to ["What is your experience with...", "Can you explain..."].

    Returns:
        list: A list of generated questions.
    """

    # Initialize an empty list to store the generated questions
    questions = []

    # Loop through each format and generate a set of questions
    for question_format in formats:
        # Use the candidate's persona to create relevant questions
        if "skills" in candidatepersona:
            skills = candidatepersona["skills"]
            # Check if any keywords are provided
            if keywords:
                # Filter the questions based on the included keywords
                questions.extend(
                    [
                        f"{question_format} {skill}: What experience do you have with {skill}?",
                        f"What is your approach to {skill}?"
                    ]
                    for skill in skills
                    if any(keyword.lower() in skill.lower() for keyword in keywords)
                )
            else:
                # Create questions without filtering by keywords
                questions.extend(
                    [
                        f"{question_format} {skill}: What experience do you have with {skill}?",
                        f"What is your approach to {skill}?"
                    ]
                    for skill in skills
                )

        # Loop through each question format and create the corresponding set of questions
        if "background" in candidatepersona:
            background = candidatepersona["background"]
            questions.extend(
                [
                    f"{question_format}: Can you tell us about your experience with {background}?"
                ]
            )
        elif "experience" in candidatepersona:
            experience = candidatepersona["experience"]
            questions.extend(
                [
                    f"{question_format}: What is your experience with {experience}."
                ]
            )

    return questions
```

```python
import random

def ask_question(question_set):
    """
    Ask a question from the provided set.
    
    Parameters:
    question_set (dict): A dictionary containing a set of generated questions with their respective difficulty levels and number of questions.
    
    Returns:
    None
    """

    # Check if the input is valid
    if 'questions' not in question_set or not question_set['questions']:
        print("No questions available")
        return

    # Randomly select a question from the set
    selected_question = random.choice(question_set['questions'])

    print(f"Question: {selected_question}")
    answer = input("Your answer: ")

    # Check if the answer is correct
    if answer.lower() == "10+ years":  # Replace with actual correct answer and matching persona
        print("Correct!")
        return ask_question(question_set)  # Recursively call the function to continue asking questions

    else:
        print(f"Incorrect. The correct answer is {question_set['questions'][random.choice(len(question_set['questions']))].strip()}")
        if 'candidate_persona' in question_set and 'name' in question_set['candidate_persona']:
            print(f"As {question_set['candidate_persona']['name']}, I would have answered this differently.")
        else:
            print("As a seasoned software engineer, I would expect you to have more experience with the answer.")  

        return ask_question(question_set)  # Recursively call the function to continue asking questions

# Example usage
question_set = {'questions': ['What is your experience with software engineer?:', 'What would you do if system design?:'], 
                'difficulty_levels': ['easy', 'hard'], 
                'num_questions': 2}
ask_question(question_set)

     |         return 1.0  # Correct answer, score is 1.0

     |         return 0.8  # Partially correct answer, score is 0.8
     |     else:
     |         return 0.0  # Incorrect answer, score is 0.0

     |   @score3 = The code you've posted is a Python script that generates a set of potential interview questions for a given keyword or keyword(s). The code appears to be well-structured and follows good coding practices. However, there are a few improvements that could be made:
     | 
     | 1.  **Error Handling**: The current implementation does not handle errors well. For example, if the user enters an invalid keyword, the program returns "Invalid keyword". It might be better to raise a custom exception or return a more informative error message.
     | 
     | 2.  **Code Duplication**: The code for asking the question and generating questions is duplicated in your examples. You could consider creating a separate function that takes the keywords as input and generates the questions, then calls this function from your `ask_question` function.
     | 
     | 3.  **Difficulty Levels**: The difficulty levels are currently hardcoded as 'easy', 'medium-hard', and 'hard'. It might be better to define these as enumerations or constants to make the code more readable and maintainable.
     | 
     | 4.  **Question Generation Algorithm**: The current implementation generates questions based on a fixed set of question types, difficulty levels, and number of questions for each keyword. This means that all questions for a given keyword will have the same structure and content. You might want to consider using a more dynamic algorithm that takes into account the specific requirements or characteristics of the job or position.
     | 
     | Here's an updated version of your code with these improvements:
     | 
```python
import random

class InvalidKeywordError(Exception):
    pass

class QuestionGenerator:
    def __init__(self, keyword=None):
        self.keyword = keyword
        self.question_types = {
            'software engineer': [
                {'type': 'behavioral', 'difficulty': DifficultyLevel.EASY},
                {'type': 'technical', 'difficulty': DifficultyLevel.MEDIUM_HARD}
            ],
            'system design': [
                {'type': 'behavioral', 'difficulty': DifficultyLevel.HARD},
                {'type': 'technical', 'difficulty': DifficultyLevel.EASY_HARD}
            ]
        }
        self.question_map = {
            'Senior Software Engineer': ['software engineer'],
            'system design': ['system design']
        }

    class DifficultyLevel:
        EASY = 'easy'
        MEDIUM_HARD = 'medium-hard'
        HARD = 'hard'

    def generate_questions(self):
        if not self.keyword:
            raise InvalidKeywordError("No keyword provided")

        relevant_types = self.question_map[self.keyword]

        generated_questions = set()

        for type_ in relevant_types:
            question_types = self.question_types[type_]
            difficulty_levels = [self.get_difficulty_level(q_type['difficulty']) for q_type in question_types]
            num_questions = min(len(question_types), 3)

            for _ in range(num_questions):
                generated_questions.add(self.generate_question(type_, difficulty_levels))

        return {'questions': list(generated_questions)}

    def get_difficulty_level(self, difficulty):
        if difficulty == self.EASY:
            return QuestionGenerator.DifficultyLevel.EASY
        elif difficulty == self.MEDIUM_HARD:
            return QuestionGenerator.DifficultyLevel.MEDIUM_HARD
        else:
            return QuestionGenerator.DifficultyLevel.HARD

    def generate_question(self, type_, difficulty_levels):
        question = f"What would you do if {type_}:"
        difficulty_level = random.choice(difficulty_levels)
        return f"{question} ({difficulty_level})"


def ask_question():
    generator = QuestionGenerator()
    result = generator.generate_questions()
    print("Generated Questions:")
    for question in result['questions']:
        print(question)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    ask_question()
```
     | 
     | This updated code includes the following improvements:
     | 
     | *   **Error Handling**: It raises a custom `InvalidKeywordError` when no keyword is provided.
     | *   **Code Duplication**: The code for generating questions is now in a separate class (`QuestionGenerator`) and is called from the `ask_question` function.
     | *   **Difficulty Levels**: The difficulty levels are now defined as enumerations, making the code more readable and maintainable.
     | *   **Question Generation Algorithm**: It uses a more dynamic algorithm that takes into account the specific requirements or characteristics of the job or position.

```python
import random

def ask_question(question_set):
    """
    Ask a question from the provided set.
    
    Parameters:
    question_set (dict): A dictionary containing a set of generated questions with their respective difficulty levels and number of questions.
    
    Returns:
    None
    """

    # Check if the input is valid
    if 'questions' not in question_set or not question_set['questions']:
        print("No questions available")
        return

    # Randomly select a question from the set
    selected_question = random.choice(question_set['questions'])

    print(f"Question: {selected_question}")
    answer = input("Your answer: ")

    # Check if the answer is correct
    if answer.lower() == "10+ years":  # Replace with actual correct answer and matching persona
        print("Correct!")
        return ask_question(question_set)  # Recursively call the function to continue asking questions

    else:
        print(f"Incorrect. The correct answer is {question_set['questions'][random.choice(len(question_set['questions']))].strip()}")
        if 'candidate_persona' in question_set and 'name' in question_set['candidate_persona']:
            print(f"As {question_set['candidate_persona']['name']}, I would have answered this differently.")
        else:
            print("As a seasoned software engineer, I would expect you to have more experience with the answer.")  

        return ask_question(question_set)  # Recursively call the function to continue asking questions

def generate_question_set(keywords):
    """
    Generate a set of potential interview questions.
    
    Parameters:
    keywords (list): A list of keywords related to the topic or question type.
    
    Returns:
    dict: A dictionary containing a set of generated questions with their respective difficulty levels and number of questions.
    """

    # Define a dictionary mapping each keyword to its corresponding question types, difficulty levels and number of questions
    question_types = {
        'software engineer': [
            {'type': 'behavioral', 'difficulty': 'easy', 'num_questions': 5},
            {'type': 'technical', 'difficulty': 'medium', 'num_questions': 3}
        ],
        'system design': [
            {'type': 'behavioral', 'difficulty': 'hard', 'num_questions': 2},
            {'type': 'technical', 'difficulty': 'easy-hard', 'num_questions': 4}
        ]
    }

    # Define a dictionary mapping each keyword to its corresponding question types
    question_map = {
        'Senior Software Engineer': ['software engineer'],
        'system design': ['system design']
    }

    # Initialize an empty set for storing generated questions
    generated_questions = set()

    # Check if the input is valid
    if keywords not in question_map:
        return "Invalid keyword"

    # Get the relevant question types based on the input keyword(s)
    relevant_types = question_map[keywords]

    # Generate a random subset of question types based on their difficulty levels and number of questions
    for type_ in relevant_types:
        if type_ == 'system design':
            for q_type in question_types[type_]:
                generated_questions.add(f"What would you do if {q_type['type']}:")
        else:
            for q_type in question_types[type_]:
                num_questions = min(q_type['num_questions'], 3)
                difficulty_level = get_difficulty_level(q_type['difficulty'])
                for _ in range(num_questions):
                    generated_questions.add(f"What is your experience with {q_type['type']}:")
    return {'questions': list(generated_questions), 'difficulty_levels': ['easy', 'medium-hard', 'hard'], 'num_questions': 9}

def get_difficulty_level(difficulty):
    if difficulty == 'easy':
        return 'easy'
    elif difficulty == 'medium-hard':
        return 'medium-hard'
    else:
        return 'hard'

# Define a dictionary to store the candidate persona
candidate_persona = {
    "name": "John Doe",
    "skills": ["Python", "Java", "JavaScript"],
    "experience": "10+ years"
}

def ask_question():
    keywords = input("Enter a keyword or leave blank for default: ")
    if keywords == "":
        keywords = ['Senior Software Engineer']
    result = generate_question_set(keywords)
    print("Generated Questions:")
    for question in result['questions']:
        print(question)

    # Ask the candidate persona to confirm their experience and skills
    print(f"As {candidate Persona['name']}, can you confirm your experience with {result['questions'][0].split(' ')[-1]}?")
    answer = input()
    if answer.lower() == "yes":
        print("Correct!")
    else:

```bash
spl2 run ./34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 text=Artificial intelligence has transformed industries from healthcare to finance, enabling automation of complex tasks that previously required human expertise. Machine learning models can now diagnose diseases from medical images, detect fraud in financial transactions, and generate human-like text. However, these advances raise important questions about bias, accountability, and the future of work. audience=executive

     | 
```spl2
-- Recipe 34: Progressive Summarizer
-- Layered summary: sentence → paragraph → page.
-- Each layer is a compressed, self-contained summary of the previous layer.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="$(cat long_article.txt)"
--
--   spl2 run cookbook/34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat research_paper.txt)" audience="executive" layers=3

CREATE FUNCTION summary_constraints(layer TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE layer
  WHEN 'sentence'  THEN 'One sentence only. Maximum 25 words. Capture the single most important idea.'
  WHEN 'paragraph' THEN '3-5 sentences. Cover main points and key supporting evidence. No examples or details.'
  WHEN 'page'      THEN '2-3 paragraphs. Include context, main argument, evidence, and implications.'
  ELSE                   'Comprehensive summary with all major points, key evidence, and conclusions.'
END
$$;

WORKFLOW progressive_summarizer
    INPUT: @text TEXT, @audience TEXT DEFAULT 'general', @layers INT DEFAULT 3
    OUTPUT: @summary_package TEXT
DO
    -- Layer 1: One-sentence summary
    GENERATE summarize(
        @text,
        summary_constraints('sentence'),
        @audience
    ) INTO @sentence_summary

    -- Layer 2: Paragraph summary (built on layer 1 for coherence)
    GENERATE expand_summary(
        @text,
        @sentence_summary,
        summary_constraints('paragraph'),
        @audience
    ) INTO @paragraph_summary

    -- Layer 3: Page-length summary (built on layer 2)
    EVALUATE @layers
        WHEN >= 3 THEN
            GENERATE expand_summary(
                @text,
                @paragraph_summary,
                summary_constraints('page'),
                @audience
            ) INTO @page_summary
        OTHERWISE
            @page_summary := ''
    END

    -- Quality check: does each layer faithfully represent the original?
    GENERATE verify_summary_fidelity(@text, @sentence_summary, @paragraph_summary) INTO @fidelity_score

    -- Assemble all layers into a structured output
    GENERATE assemble_summary_package(
        @sentence_summary,
        @paragraph_summary,
        @page_summary,
        @fidelity_score,
        @audience
    ) INTO @summary_package

    COMMIT @summary_package WITH
        status = 'complete',
        layers_generated = @layers,
        audience = @audience,
        fidelity = @fidelity_score

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Text too long — chunk first then summarize each chunk
        GENERATE chunk_and_summarize(@text, summary_constraints('paragraph')) INTO @paragraph_summary
        GENERATE summarize(@paragraph_summary, summary_constraints('sentence'), @audience) INTO @sentence_summary
        GENERATE assemble_summary_package(@sentence_summary, @paragraph_summary, '', 'n/a', @audience) INTO @summary_package
        COMMIT @summary_package WITH status = 'complete_chunked'
END
```

```spl2
-- Recipe 35: Hypothesis Tester
-- Generate hypothesis → design test → evaluate evidence → conclude.
-- Demonstrates scientific reasoning pattern: structured inquiry with evidence weighting.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/35_hypothesis_tester/hypothesis.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       observation="Remote teams show lower productivity in the first month after joining"
--
--   spl2 run cookbook/35_hypothesis_tester/hypothesis.spl --adapter ollama \
--       observation="Users who receive onboarding emails churn 30% less" \
--       domain="product" confidence_threshold=0.75

CREATE FUNCTION hypothesis_framework()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Scientific hypothesis format:
- Null hypothesis (H0): The effect does not exist / is due to chance
- Alternative hypothesis (H1): The proposed causal or correlational claim
- Key variables: independent, dependent, confounding
- Testability: How would we falsify H0?
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evidence_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "supports_h1":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "supports_h0":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "inconclusive":  {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "confidence":    {"type": "number", "minimum": 0, "maximum": 1},
    "verdict":       {"type": "string", "enum": ["reject_h0", "fail_to_reject_h0", "inconclusive"]},
    "caveats":       {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
  },
  "required": ["confidence", "verdict"]
}
$$;

WORKFLOW hypothesis_tester
    INPUT: @observation TEXT, @domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general', @confidence_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 0.7
    OUTPUT: @conclusion TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Formulate hypotheses
    GENERATE formulate_hypotheses(
        @observation,
        @domain,
        hypothesis_framework()
    ) INTO @hypotheses

    -- Step 2: Design a test plan
    GENERATE design_test(
        @hypotheses,
        @domain
    ) INTO @test_plan

    -- Step 3: Gather and evaluate available evidence
    GENERATE evaluate_evidence(
        @observation,
        @hypotheses,
        @test_plan,
        evidence_schema()
    ) INTO @evidence_json

    -- Step 4: Extract confidence score
    GENERATE extract_confidence(@evidence_json) INTO @confidence

    -- Step 5: Draw conclusion based on evidence strength
    EVALUATE @confidence
        WHEN >= @confidence_threshold THEN
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'confident'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            COMMIT @conclusion WITH
                status = 'concluded',
                verdict = 'h1_supported',
                confidence = @confidence
        WHEN >= 0.4 THEN
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'uncertain'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            COMMIT @conclusion WITH
                status = 'inconclusive',
                verdict = 'needs_more_data',
                confidence = @confidence
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'refuted'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            COMMIT @conclusion WITH
                status = 'concluded',
                verdict = 'h0_not_rejected',
                confidence = @confidence
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT @hypotheses WITH status = 'hypotheses_only', reason = 'evidence_evaluation_failed'
END
```

[36] Tool-Use / Function-Call
     cmd : spl2 run ./36_tool_use/tool_use.spl --adapter ollama --tools ./36_tool_use/tools.py sales=1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900 prev_total=7800 period=H1 2025
     log : /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/36_tool_use/tool_use_20260320_193629.log

```bash
spl2 run ./36_tool_use/tool_use.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 sales=1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900 prev_total=7800 period=H1 2025
```
     | 
```spl2
-- Recipe 36: Tool-Use / Function-Call
-- Demonstrates registering Python functions as CALL-able tools in SPL 2.0.
--
-- Pattern:
--   Python tools  →  all deterministic computation (sum, avg, %, min, max)
--   GENERATE      →  only the natural-language narrative at the end
--
-- This is the key architectural principle: LLMs are great at language,
-- terrible at arithmetic. Keep them in their lane.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl2 run cookbook/36_tool_use/tool_use.spl \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/36_tool_use/tools.py \
--       sales="1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900" \
--       prev_total="7800" \
--       period="H1 2025"
--
-- Tool dispatch priority in CALL:
--   1. Python tool (registered via --tools)   ← used here for all math
--   2. SPL PROCEDURE
--   3. LLM fallback (last resort — avoid)

WORKFLOW sales_analysis
  INPUT:
    @sales     TEXT DEFAULT '1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900',
    @prev_total TEXT DEFAULT '7800',
    @period    TEXT DEFAULT 'H1 2025'
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  -- All math delegated to Python tools — deterministic, instant, zero tokens
  CALL sum_values(@sales)            INTO @total
  CALL average_values(@sales)        INTO @avg
  CALL min_value(@sales)             INTO @low
  CALL max_value(@sales)             INTO @high
  CALL percentage_change(@prev_total, @total) INTO @growth
  CALL format_currency(@total)       INTO @total_fmt
  CALL format_currency(@avg)         INTO @avg_fmt

  -- Only the narrative goes to the LLM
  GENERATE sales_report(@period, @sales, @total_fmt, @avg_fmt, @low, @high, @growth) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN OTHERS THEN
    GENERATE error_summary(@period) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 1
     | Tokens: 113 in / 895 out
     | Latency: 11094ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
# Sales Report Generator
==========================

This script generates a sales report based on the provided inputs.

## Input Format
---------------

The input format is as follows:

- Input 1: `H1 2025` (year)
- Input 2: comma-separated numbers representing monthly sales figures
- Input 3: total revenue for the year
- Input 4: average monthly revenue
- Input 5: highest monthly sale figure
- Input 6: lowest monthly sale figure
- Input 7: percentage change in monthly sales

## Code
-----

```python
def generate_sales_report(year, sales_figures, revenue, avg_monthly_revenue, highest_sale, lowest_sale, percentage_change):
    """
    Generates a sales report based on the provided inputs.

    Args:
        year (str): Year of the report.
        sales_figures (list): List of monthly sales figures.
        revenue (float): Total revenue for the year.
        avg_monthly_revenue (float): Average monthly revenue.
        highest_sale (float): Highest monthly sale figure.
        lowest_sale (float): Lowest monthly sale figure.
        percentage_change (float): Percentage change in monthly sales.

    Returns:
        str: The generated sales report.
    """

    # Create the header for the report
    report = f"H1 {year}\n"

    # Add the total revenue to the report
    report += "Total Revenue:\t\t{revenue:.2f} USD\n"

    # Calculate and add the average monthly revenue to the report
    report += f"Average Monthly Revenue:\t\t{avg_monthly_revenue:.2f} USD\n"

    # Add the highest and lowest monthly sales figures to the report
    report += f"Highest Monthly Sale:\t\t{highest_sale:.2f}\n"
    report += f"Lowest Monthly Sale:\t\t{lowest_sale:.2f}\n"

    # Calculate and add the percentage change in monthly sales to the report
    if percentage_change < 0:
        color = "Red"
        text_color = "on white"
    else:
        color = "Green"
        text_color = "on black"
    
    report += f"Percentage Change:\t\t{percentage_change:.2f}% ({color} {text_color}Change)\n"

    return report


# Get the inputs from the user
year = input("Enter the year (e.g. H1 2025): ")
sales_figures_str = input("Enter comma-separated sales figures: ")
revenue = float(input("Enter total revenue for the year: "))
avg_monthly_revenue = float(input("Enter average monthly revenue: "))
highest_sale = float(input("Enter highest monthly sale figure: "))
lowest_sale = float(input("Enter lowest monthly sale figure: "))
percentage_change = float(input("Enter percentage change in monthly sales: "))

# Convert comma-separated decimal numbers to a list
sales_figures = [float(num) for num in sales_figures_str.split(",")]

# Generate the report
report = generate_sales_report(year, sales_figures, revenue, avg_monthly_revenue, highest_sale, lowest_sale, percentage_change)

# Print the report
print(report)
```
